  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',

        state: 'apple_oauth_state_193d2be5-b47d-4dd0-bd65-2d04dc0ef717'

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer2153947307814526197">The first book to deal with the problems of communicating to a skeptical, media-blitzed public, <i>Positioning</i> describes a revolutionary approach to creating a "position" in a prospective customer's mind-one that reflects a company's own strengths and weaknesses as well as those of its competitors. Writing in their trademark witty, fast-paced style, advertising gurus Ries and</span>
  <span id="freeText2153947307814526197" style="display:none">The first book to deal with the problems of communicating to a skeptical, media-blitzed public, <i>Positioning</i> describes a revolutionary approach to creating a "position" in a prospective customer's mind-one that reflects a company's own strengths and weaknesses as well as those of its competitors. Writing in their trademark witty, fast-paced style, advertising gurus Ries and Trout explain how to:<br /><br /><br />Make and position an industry leader so that its name and message wheedles its way into the collective subconscious of your market-and stays there<br />Position a follower so that it can occupy a niche not claimed by the leader<br />Avoid letting a second product ride on the coattails of an established one.<br />Positioning also shows you how to:<br /><br /><br />Use leading ad agency techniques to capture the biggest market share and become a household name<br />Build your strategy around your competition's weaknesses<br />Reposition a strong competitor and create a weak spot<br />Use your present position to its best advantage<br />Choose the best name for your product<br />Determine when-and why-less is more<br />Analyze recent trends that affect your positioning.<br />Ries and Trout provide many valuable case histories and penetrating analyses of some of the most phenomenal successes and failures in advertising history. Revised to reflect significant developments in the five years since its original publication, <i>Positioning</i> is required reading for anyone in business today.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2153947307814526197" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>
</div>

    <div id=buyButtonContainer class="uitext buttons buyableFeature u-marginTopXSmall u-paddingBottomXSmall u-bottomGrayBorder" data-book-id=760025 >

    <div class="u-positionRelative">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/173569482?book_show_action=true">Jun 04, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Milla Nezlina" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Milla Nezlina" href="/user/show/4764072-milla-nezlina">Milla Nezlina</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4764072-milla-nezlina?shelf=business">business</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer173569482" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15701557629630600268">The whole book can be boiled down to 2 key ideas: <br/>- The brand that gets in the mind of the consumer first wins. Who makes the best soda? Coke! The best small cars? VW with its Beetle! The best round chips? Pringles! Tip: Find a narrow niche and hold it tight! <br/>- Line extension is bad (Xerox producing computers in bad, Chevrolet making small cars is bad, even Pantene producing body foam is bad). Tip to companies: Never stray from the business where you are the leader! <br/><br/>I read the updated edition of</span>
  <span id="freeText15701557629630600268" style="display:none">The whole book can be boiled down to 2 key ideas: <br />- The brand that gets in the mind of the consumer first wins. Who makes the best soda? Coke! The best small cars? VW with its Beetle! The best round chips? Pringles! Tip: Find a narrow niche and hold it tight! <br />- Line extension is bad (Xerox producing computers in bad, Chevrolet making small cars is bad, even Pantene producing body foam is bad). Tip to companies: Never stray from the business where you are the leader! <br /><br />I read the updated edition of the book that was originally published in the ate 70's. It is fun to read the comments of the authors like: "30 years ago we thought that Time was an awful name for the news magazine, but now we think that it is better than the generic name Newsweek." - it shows that everything is relative in the world of advertising. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15701557629630600268" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1413147624" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1413147624" />
    <a title="Chad Warner" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1586355-chad-warner"><img alt="Chad Warner" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1546011101p2/1586355.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1413147624?book_show_action=true">Oct 10, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chad Warner" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chad Warner" href="/user/show/1586355-chad-warner">Chad Warner</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Chad by: </span>
      Luke Grill
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1586355-chad-warner?shelf=business">business</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1586355-chad-warner?shelf=marketing">marketing</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1586355-chad-warner?shelf=branding">branding</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1413147624" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12730631278463683665">This book tells how to establish a position for your products and company in the minds of prospects. The authors say that the essence of positioning is to make your brand name stand for the generic (e.g., Kleenex).<br/><br/>I like the advice to start with the position you already have (what you’re already known for), and work to improve from there.<br/><br/>It’s mostly applicable to large companies with large advertising budgets, but most principles can apply to smaller businesses. It’s mostly applicable to product</span>
  <span id="freeText12730631278463683665" style="display:none">This book tells how to establish a position for your products and company in the minds of prospects. The authors say that the essence of positioning is to make your brand name stand for the generic (e.g., Kleenex).<br /><br />I like the advice to start with the position you already have (what you’re already known for), and work to improve from there.<br /><br />It’s mostly applicable to large companies with large advertising budgets, but most principles can apply to smaller businesses. It’s mostly applicable to products, not services, but most lessons can apply to services.<br /><br />I read this to learn more about positioning <a href="https://optimwise.com" rel="nofollow noopener">my web design agency, OptimWise</a>. I read it because it was recommended by a local restaurateur. I’ve also seen it on lists of marketing books.<br /><br />Below are my notes.<br /><br /><strong>Pricing</strong><br />“Charging high prices is not the way to get rich. Being the first to (1) establish the high price position (2) with a valid product story (3) in a category where consumers are receptive to a high-priced brand is the secret of success.”<br /><br />“The place to establish the high price is in the ads, not in the store. The price (high or low) is as much a feature of the product as anything else.”<br /><br />“There should be no surprises in the store. Your ads don’t have to quote exact prices, although sometimes that’s a good thing to do. What they should do, however, is to clearly position your brand in a particular price category.”<br /><br /><strong>The Power of the Name</strong><br />A name should begin the positioning process by telling the product’s major benefit. For example, Head &amp; Shoulders Shampoo, or DieHard batteries. But the name shouldn’t be so close to the product itself to become generic. It should be strong, generic-like, and descriptive.<br /><br />In names, stick with common descriptive words (e.g., Spray ‘n Wash) and avoid coined words (e.g., Qyx).<br /><br />Prevent customer confusion by putting the product/service you’re known for in your company name. For example, change Continental Corp. to Continental Insurance.<br /><br />“A name is a rubber band. It will stretch, but not beyond a certain point. Furthermore, the more you stretch a name, the weaker it becomes.”<br /><br /><strong>The Free-Ride Trap</strong><br />“One name can’t stand for two distinctly different products. When one goes up, the other goes down.” Use different names for different products.<br /><br /><strong>The Line-Extension Trap</strong><br />The more products hung on a brand name, the less meaning the name has to the average consumer.<br /><br />Offering a step-down product (lower end version) hurts the prestige of the original.<br /><br /><strong>Positioning Your Business</strong><br />“Your company’s name ought to stand for something within your industry.”<br /><br />Don’t try to position based on having better people (staff). Buyers won’t believe it.<br /><br />“The solution to a positioning problem is usually found in the prospect’s mind, not in the product.”<br /><br />“Find a way into the mind by hooking your product, service, or concept to what’s already there.”<br /><br />“If you can start with a strongly held perception, you’ll be that much ahead in your efforts to establish your own position.”<br /><br />“Start with what the prospect is already willing to give you.” Emphasize your perceived advantages. “Instead of asking what you are, you ask what position you already own in the mind of the prospect”<br /><br />You find your position by studying your competition, not yourself.<br /><br />Smaller businesses should position against large competitors, because the large competitors are in the minds of prospects. In ads, exploit their weaknesses.<br /><br />“A positioning exercise is a search for the obvious. Those are the easiest concepts to communicate because they make the most sense to the recipient of a message. Unfortunately, obvious concepts are also the most difficult to recognize and to sell.”<br /><br />“The big winners in business and in life are those people who have found open positions near the center of the spectrum. Not at the edge.” For example, “You must be slightly conservative in a field of liberals or slightly liberal in a field of conservatives.”<br /><br />“Prospects don’t buy, they choose … among brands … The merit, or lack of merit, of your brand is not nearly as important as your position among the possible choices.<br /><br />“Often to create a viable position, you must reposition another brand or even an entire category of product. As Tylenol did to aspirin, for example.”</span>
  <a data-text-id="12730631278463683665" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_14437194" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/14437194" />
    <a title="Chris Herdt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/157265-chris-herdt"><img alt="Chris Herdt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1415923375p2/157265.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/14437194?book_show_action=true">Feb 03, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chris Herdt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chris Herdt" href="/user/show/157265-chris-herdt">Chris Herdt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Chris by: </span>
      Prof. Mike Rechtiene
  </div>


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer14437194" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3454992671268823011">This book feels like it was composed via cell phone text messages. Many of the paragraphs are a single sentence.<br/><br/>The main idea is that you cannot make in-roads in an existing market by attacking the top dog. The top dog, the market leader, will beat you every time. Instead, the authors argue that you have to position your product/company/person in the mind of the consumer, in relation to the market leader. And forcing your idealized position on the mind of the consumer won't work, so your positio</span>
  <span id="freeText3454992671268823011" style="display:none">This book feels like it was composed via cell phone text messages. Many of the paragraphs are a single sentence.<br /><br />The main idea is that you cannot make in-roads in an existing market by attacking the top dog. The top dog, the market leader, will beat you every time. Instead, the authors argue that you have to position your product/company/person in the mind of the consumer, in relation to the market leader. And forcing your idealized position on the mind of the consumer won't work, so your position often starts with the existing public perception of your product/service/idea.<br /><br />The example used numerous times throughout the book is Avis. Avis was not going to convince the public that they were the #1 auto rental agency--everyone knew that Hertz was #1. Hence the campaign: "Avis is only No. 2 in rent-a-cars, so why go with us? We try harder."<br /><br />The authors are extremely cocksure, in sometimes amusing ways (my edition is from 1986). For example, they ridicule Arm &amp; Hammer's attempts to sell baking soda personal hygiene products with a Phyllis Diller joke. Who is laughing now, Ries and Trout?<br /><br />I read this for my marketing class, but it is an interesting book, and a quick read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3454992671268823011" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5038680-eric-lin?shelf=nonfiction">nonfiction</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer606381273" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15198839447324936117">I don't read books about marketing. Ever. It was pretty interesting though, since the trends he describes can definitely be observed in action.<br/><br/>However, Ries raised a lot of red flags with how certain he sounded about his assessments. He says things like, "a better name for product X? *name he just came up with*". Sometimes, he makes these statements without much supporting evidence. Anyway, it wasn't a huge part of the book, but he sounded so sure about everything, and that really off-putting fo</span>
  <span id="freeText15198839447324936117" style="display:none">I don't read books about marketing. Ever. It was pretty interesting though, since the trends he describes can definitely be observed in action.<br /><br />However, Ries raised a lot of red flags with how certain he sounded about his assessments. He says things like, "a better name for product X? *name he just came up with*". Sometimes, he makes these statements without much supporting evidence. Anyway, it wasn't a huge part of the book, but he sounded so sure about everything, and that really off-putting for me. A certain degree of uncertainty and humility about his assessments would have been more realistic, but I realize that Al Ries is getting paid, in large part, for his conviction.<br /><br />Also think this book needs an update, given how much the world has changed since this book was written. It's probably true that the same forces are at work in today's market, but a lot of his examples were lost on me.<br /><br />Still, an interesting read, that gave me a completely new perspective on the value of good advertising in the marketplace.</span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer910776417" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1211390442939387302">This is a good book in need of an update. I read it for a grad school class and while it was interesting in parts, it was very much undergraduate level (Marketing 101) information. The biggest issue I had with it is that every example is out of date. He mentions companies that have now closed down and talks about the great things that they are doing - for example, he talks about how Newsweek is a much better magazine name than Time and will surely be more successful because of it. Since we all k</span>
  <span id="freeText1211390442939387302" style="display:none">This is a good book in need of an update. I read it for a grad school class and while it was interesting in parts, it was very much undergraduate level (Marketing 101) information. The biggest issue I had with it is that every example is out of date. He mentions companies that have now closed down and talks about the great things that they are doing - for example, he talks about how Newsweek is a much better magazine name than Time and will surely be more successful because of it. Since we all know that did not end up being true, it makes you wary of his advice throughout the book. There's another part where he mentions that George Bush is a handsome man and I thought "OK, sure, W isn't too bad, I guess?" and then you realize he means George Bush Senior and the whole book seems like a relic from another time. If publishers would update it with new information on social media and some more modern examples, I could see this getting new life as a must read for students. </span>

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review910776417">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer407246671" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer12685035995828517577">The opening chapters are very blowhardy: I nearly threw down the book in disgust. But when the authors finally get going they're golden (if fairly dated). Their insights on how brands convey meaning, especially how they fail to carry all the meaning companies intend them to when they create line extensions, all of that stuff is great and makes me want to re-read this in a few years (or maybe grab the audiobook) to see how the knowledge has settled. Absolutely essential reading for anybody trying</span>
  <span id="freeText12685035995828517577" style="display:none">The opening chapters are very blowhardy: I nearly threw down the book in disgust. But when the authors finally get going they're golden (if fairly dated). Their insights on how brands convey meaning, especially how they fail to carry all the meaning companies intend them to when they create line extensions, all of that stuff is great and makes me want to re-read this in a few years (or maybe grab the audiobook) to see how the knowledge has settled. Absolutely essential reading for anybody trying to figure out what to name their product, or what to do about the next product they want to launch while keeping an existing product in market.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer15596541717095828425">I read this book to help enhance the growth of my start-up ladies leisurewear brand, Broads. I was hoping to learn more about creating a solid position in a marketplace that is difficult to 'break' into. </span>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer274420324982586898">I'm not sure it's even four stars. I've really struggled through the bigger half of the book, but maybe its just me bored of reading advertising/marketing stories and preferring anything else. <br/>For those who want a short version, there's an old interview "The mind is the ultimate battlefield" with Al Ries - it perfectly served me since the university days and gave a good enough understanding of what the big idea is.<br/>There are certain values of the long read:<br/>- historical overview of marketing lands</span>
  <span id="freeText274420324982586898" style="display:none">I'm not sure it's even four stars. I've really struggled through the bigger half of the book, but maybe its just me bored of reading advertising/marketing stories and preferring anything else. <br />For those who want a short version, there's an old interview "The mind is the ultimate battlefield" with Al Ries - it perfectly served me since the university days and gave a good enough understanding of what the big idea is.<br />There are certain values of the long read:<br />- historical overview of marketing landscape 40 years ago - there's always some charm in those stories. Oh the days when Western Union was innovating in the telegram's field or Xerox was hoping to take more than only the copy machines market. Lovely<br />- good mind exercise at trying to come up with current examples to prove or disapprove the points. Disapproving is, sure, more fun thing to do. <br />- another good exercise is rethinking what still works and makes sense - or how it can be made to work. <br /><br />From the intriguing parts, there's a chapter on personal positioning. Who could tell these days that "chap, there are six horses you can ride to success - but pick a good one, because it's not the rider, but the fastest horse who wins" Beat that. <br />And all the worries what the huge information overload our world suffered 40 y ago. I feel them.<br /><br />So ok, it's worth 4 stars. <br /></span>

<div id="review_3447234259" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3447234259" />
    <a title="Dhruv Shah" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/51831072-dhruv-shah"><img alt="Dhruv Shah" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1595222009p2/51831072.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3447234259?book_show_action=true">Jul 23, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dhruv Shah" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dhruv Shah" href="/user/show/51831072-dhruv-shah">Dhruv Shah</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/51831072-dhruv-shah?shelf=business">business</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3447234259" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14714315321005908953">TL;DR<br/>(Background: I am an MBA Marketing student from India)<br/>A few essential concepts in a 200+ pages book that is dated and only relevant for big companies. Almost every single example mentioned in the book is of big American companies from the 60s or 70s. As a person born and raised in India, I have to say I was not familiar with the majority of the examples. If you're the same, you might not fully understand every concept explained in the book.<br/><br/>I picked up this book after reading many good revie</span>
  <span id="freeText14714315321005908953" style="display:none">TL;DR<br />(Background: I am an MBA Marketing student from India)<br />A few essential concepts in a 200+ pages book that is dated and only relevant for big companies. Almost every single example mentioned in the book is of big American companies from the 60s or 70s. As a person born and raised in India, I have to say I was not familiar with the majority of the examples. If you're the same, you might not fully understand every concept explained in the book.<br /><br />I picked up this book after reading many good reviews on multiple websites (Goodreads, Amazon, Twitter, etc.). Some of which put this book into top 5 marketing books of all time. So I was very excited before starting this book. However, I started the book, and I kept waiting for the exciting part to come, but it didn't. There were some essential concepts here and there and some iconic marketing campaigns which I wasn't aware of. However, as I mentioned before, it's a dated book with examples of American products from the 60s and 70s. Even the 20th-anniversary edition with authors' comments is 20 years old. Needless to say, marketing has changed a lot since those days and many ideas mentioned in the book just don't work anymore.<br />This brings me to the second biggest problem that I found in the book: the lessons and ideas in the book seemed as if they are derived from a limited set of very successful or failed products. And I firmly believe that they can not be generalised to other industries, geographies and time periods. This is evident in comments made by authors in the 20th-anniversary edition, where they have admitted of being horribly wrong about multiple predictions made in 1980 about big brands. And after 20 years of that edition, many more ideas and predictions have proved to be incorrect. So overall, I would not place this book in the must-reads for Marketing considering its relevance and the fact that it only deals with a fraction of the entire marketing process.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14714315321005908953" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2705068089" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2705068089" />
    <a title="Martti" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14012525-martti"><img alt="Martti" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1351375872p2/14012525.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2705068089?book_show_action=true">Feb 06, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Martti" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Martti" href="/user/show/14012525-martti">Martti</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audio CD 9781932378252" href="/book/show/1341125.Positioning">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2705068089" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14783191521385267066">The brand that gets in the mind of the consumer first wins. <br/><br/>Line extension is bad (Xerox expanding into computers with the same product name "Xerox" is not recommended). “One name can’t stand for two distinctly different products. When one goes up, the other goes down.” The more products hung on a brand name, the less meaning the name has to the average consumer.<br/><br/>How to establish a position for your products and company in the minds of prospects. The authors say that the essence of positioning is</span>
  <span id="freeText14783191521385267066" style="display:none">The brand that gets in the mind of the consumer first wins. <br /><br />Line extension is bad (Xerox expanding into computers with the same product name "Xerox" is not recommended). “One name can’t stand for two distinctly different products. When one goes up, the other goes down.” The more products hung on a brand name, the less meaning the name has to the average consumer.<br /><br />How to establish a position for your products and company in the minds of prospects. The authors say that the essence of positioning is to make your brand name stand for the generic (e.g., Kleenex).<br /><br />The main idea is that you cannot make in-roads in an existing market by attacking the top dog. The top dog, the market leader, will beat you every time. Instead, the authors argue that you have to position your product/company/person in the mind of the consumer, in relation to the market leader.<br /><br />I would summarize this book as a view into corporate marketroids headspace. </span>
  <a data-text-id="14783191521385267066" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1947478991" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4898827915636944610">This book talks about how to stand out in an overcrowded field--and it's not by merely being good at what you do. Yes, you have to be excellent. But there are many others who are excellent as well. He talks about "finding the hole" where others are not (e.g. the masculine-style perfume for women, Charlie). Or by deliberately playing off of the brand position of another (e.g. Jamaica as the Hawaii of the Caribbean). He shares about the power of a clear name and the trap of line extension.<br/><br/>It's a s</span>
  <span id="freeText4898827915636944610" style="display:none">This book talks about how to stand out in an overcrowded field--and it's not by merely being good at what you do. Yes, you have to be excellent. But there are many others who are excellent as well. He talks about "finding the hole" where others are not (e.g. the masculine-style perfume for women, Charlie). Or by deliberately playing off of the brand position of another (e.g. Jamaica as the Hawaii of the Caribbean). He shares about the power of a clear name and the trap of line extension.<br /><br />It's a strong book and the only reason it's four stars and not five is that it's old enough to be a little out of touch with modern readers and has no comments on the wild world of online business/brands today. Still worth reading for all leaders and innovators.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2386287345" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6640556869161002692">A classic whose concepts are covered more succinctly in the authors’ follow up Immutable Laws of Marketing. There are more detailed examples here, but it suffers from even more dated company and trend references (drugs and airlines that haven’t existed since the 70s, a positioning plan for a Western Union service, and hailing cable TV as the next great frontier).<br/><br/>I want to applaud the concepts, which I think are timeless. But I keep getting tripped up by the ancient references.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3346071719" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11121062622352138306"><br/>055-Positioning-The Battle for Your Mind-Al Ries-Business-1981<br/>Barack<br/><br/>——What matters is not what kind of person you are now, but what kind of person you want to be in the future. We need to think carefully about where we are now and where we will go in the future.<br/><br/>Positioning: The Battle for Your Mind, first published in 1981. An economic book. The "positioning" marketing concept it puts forward has had a huge impact on the entire marketing community.<br/><br/>AL Ries was born in Atlanta, the USA in 1926. He</span>
  <span id="freeText11121062622352138306" style="display:none"><br />055-Positioning-The Battle for Your Mind-Al Ries-Business-1981<br />Barack<br /><br />——What matters is not what kind of person you are now, but what kind of person you want to be in the future. We need to think carefully about where we are now and where we will go in the future.<br /><br />Positioning: The Battle for Your Mind, first published in 1981. An economic book. The "positioning" marketing concept it puts forward has had a huge impact on the entire marketing community.<br /><br />AL Ries was born in Atlanta, the USA in 1926. He studied at Diebold University. Representative works: "Positioning", "Life Positioning", "Marketing Revolution", etc.<br /><br />Each of us has thought about what we want to be like in the eyes of others. This kind of consciously shaping personal image can be called "manufacturing" or "positioning".<br /><br />We often talk about personal core competitiveness. So, what is the individual's core competitiveness? It should be condensed into a label or impression. That is, when others think of a word, they will think of you as soon as possible.<br /><br />For example, you think your biggest advantage is reliability. So, when the people around you think of finding a reliable person, is the first thing person thinks of you? Or maybe you think your greatest strength is generosity. So, if the person around you needs to seek a generous person, is the first thing that comes to their minds you? If not, then you may need to continue to strengthen this trait.<br /><br />We can cite many examples around us. When we mention social networking, we think of Tencent, when we mention e-commerce, we think of Alibaba. Of course, it is difficult for us to distinguish in the end because they are positioned, so they succeed. It was because of their success that we only knew their location.<br /><br />If a person wants to maximize his value, he needs to find his place in society. Should I enter a certain position in the officialdom, or enter a shopping mall or academia? After determining the basic direction, we need to think about which functional department of an enterprise we should be in, whether it is technology, product, or sales. Only by embodying the core strength of the individual in the proper position can the value of the individual be maximized.<br /><br />We need to ask ourselves, what is our core competitiveness? At which point, I can vow to ensure that no one I know can surpass me in this respect.<br /><br />In the process of starting a business, I once got a suggestion. Can you clarify your product in one sentence or within 10 seconds? This is a very valuable and difficult attempt. And this definition needs to be constantly revised.<br /><br />With a slight extension on this basis, I try to think about such a problem. Can I explain my lifelong pursuit in one sentence or 10 seconds? If it is not clear, it means that I may not have figured out how I should spend my only and precious life.<br /><br />This is like fighting, focusing on superior forces to focus on one target. If the forces are dispersed on a long line of defense, then on each line of defense, oneself is in a weak position. For war, the difficulty lies in choosing which bunker to attack. For business, the difficulty lies in where to place your focus.<br /><br />So if you are the number one player in the market, you can try to educate users, but if the market has been educated, then you better do things according to the user's inertial thinking, try to change the old ideas is often more difficult than planting a new idea.<br /><br />During my internship on WeChat, I realized that one of the biggest dilemmas currently facing the WeChat public platform is that people pay too much attention to the WeChat public account so that their attention is greatly diluted. A person is either unwilling to pay attention to the new WeChat public account or read almost no articles on the WeChat public account that has been followed and may only look at the title.<br /><br />We need to make such an assumption, that is, when you first meet a stranger, what kind of impression do you want to make to him? You better spend 80% of your energy on this. When you finish the meeting, if he can put an equal sign between you and a certain word, then you have almost approached and positioned successfully.<br /><br />Another problem we will encounter in life is writing resumes. As a fresh graduate of 24 years old. There may be many things that can be written on a resume. But which ones should I choose to write? Before writing, I need to ask myself this question: If I am an interviewer, what impression do I have after I read my resume for 10 seconds?<br /><br />People also have this obvious tendency emotionally. For individuals, perhaps the most profound memory is still the first love. Although first love may not be perfect. But precisely because she occupied her soul for the first time, it made it difficult for a person to forget for life.<br /><br />We can also find many examples of the first to enter the market but were replaced by latecomers. In these examples, the first-mover advantage is offset by some wrong decisions by the decision-maker. Although consumers may still have emotions about the brand, the poor performance of the product makes consumers finally choose to embrace the new brand.<br /><br />Even for the same fact, different people will see different emphasis. People tend to see him want to see. Perhaps this is why the iPhone ’s annual conference has only a few versions. It doesn’t provide parallel and diversified product versions. If the iPhone launches 10 or even 20 different models of products every year to adapt to different needs, it may also succeed. But that is another completely different path.<br /><br />I was watching the first iPhone conference in 2007. Steve Jobs' introduction to the iPhone is impressive. He needs to associate such a new product with an old product before he can penetrate people's minds.<br /><br />Most consumers do not investigate the market share of this product before purchasing. They just make judgments based on their impressions.<br /><br />In 2020, Chinese college graduates reached 8.74 million. For most people, finding a job is difficult. One of the core problems is that he cannot achieve a specific point on which he is better than most of his peers. If everyone is similar in every aspect, naturally, there will be no competitive advantage.<br /><br />Everyone wants to occupy the city. But if the city is already occupied, then it is not necessarily a good idea to challenge the city directly. It may be a better idea to focus on the countryside where the enemy is weak.<br /><br />So, when you want to enter a field, think about whether there is a strong player with a strong position. You must find a place where you are different from him, and take this difference as your biggest selling point.<br /><br />There is a sentence in the New Testament. For whosoever hath, to him shall be given, and he shall have more abundance: but whosoever hath not, from him shall be taken away even that he hath.<br /><br />So, we say, step by step. One step is wrong, and every step will be wrong. Although success cannot be ensured, it is crucial to seize the first-mover advantage.<br /><br />16/01/29<br />20/05/20<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11121062622352138306" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_749320015" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/749320015" />
    <a title="Puja" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6196787-puja"><img alt="Puja" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1453228949p2/6196787.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/749320015?book_show_action=true">Oct 25, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Puja" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Puja" href="/user/show/6196787-puja">Puja</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6196787-puja?shelf=advertising">advertising</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6196787-puja?shelf=brand-management">brand-management</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer749320015" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14904974921743831455">This book was a curriculum requirement in my Integrated Marketing Communications program at Emerson College; it was discussed and treated with utmost importance in each Advertising class I had. A marketing classic, 'Positioning: The Battle for your mind' helped me understand the concept of branding even better, and for that I am thankful to my professor Bill Anderson. <br/><br/>So, what is positioning ? In the minds of the consumers there are different needs. And for each need there exists a solution out </span>
  <span id="freeText14904974921743831455" style="display:none">This book was a curriculum requirement in my Integrated Marketing Communications program at Emerson College; it was discussed and treated with utmost importance in each Advertising class I had. A marketing classic, 'Positioning: The Battle for your mind' helped me understand the concept of branding even better, and for that I am thankful to my professor Bill Anderson. <br /><br />So, what is positioning ? In the minds of the consumers there are different needs. And for each need there exists a solution out there. And in the minds of the consumers each solution is ranked according to its perceived value - basically if your perception is that X fulfills your A need better than Y does, then X is on a higher rung of the A ladder - it is positioned better than Y. The book's premise is that X reached that top position by being the first to get into your mind. And of course by managing to fulfill its brand promise. Brands should strive to reach the number one spot by getting to the consumers before any other brand does. The reason why people think of Beetle when they think of small cars is that Volkswagen was the first company to create that need (or its perception) for a small car and provide a solution for the same.<br /><br />The other main premise of the book is that line extension doesn't work and companies should stick to what their original purpose is. By spreading themselves too thin and extending their product lines, they can confuse the consumers and deplete their brand value. However, since the book has been published, much has changed in the world of marketing. There have been cases where businesses have defied the line extension rule that the authors spell out, and managed to succeed.<br /><br />The book may seem a bit outdated, particularly with the examples, but it's relevant nevertheless. It is a short read, and the concise presentation with ample cases make it easily accessible even to people from non-marketing backgrounds - they can use it as a guide to define or redefine their business strategy.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14904974921743831455" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer98603156" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10702296859638555881">In my job, there's some downtime. It's feast or famine writing. So our superiors have requested that in these periods of nothingness we delve into some reading with the goal of honing some sort of skills, i.e., professional development. Ok. Fine. <i>Positioning: The Battle for Your Mind</i> is one of the books on our list.<br/><br/>This is certainly not a book I would have ever picked up on my own, and I've yet to determine if it's made an impression on me. Hmmmmm. Well, yeah, I guess it has a little bit. Some p</span>
  <span id="freeText10702296859638555881" style="display:none">In my job, there's some downtime. It's feast or famine writing. So our superiors have requested that in these periods of nothingness we delve into some reading with the goal of honing some sort of skills, i.e., professional development. Ok. Fine. <i>Positioning: The Battle for Your Mind</i> is one of the books on our list.<br /><br />This is certainly not a book I would have ever picked up on my own, and I've yet to determine if it's made an impression on me. Hmmmmm. Well, yeah, I guess it has a little bit. Some points stand out. I feel like a lot of it is common sense, but then again when you're an intuitive person and you make your living as a persuasive writer, maybe it's just that I've already grasped a lot of the points the book is shooting to make.<br /><br />This is a very quick read. No sentence is longer than eight words. A fifth grader could get through it. Whether or not said fifth grader would absorb the material is another matter, however. It's also an old book, published in 1981, I think. And it dates itself big time, even in the 20th anniversary edition I read. The reference to Tab being a better selling product than the failure Diet Pepsi was amusing. <br /><br />If you're at all interested in how the advertising or successful politicking mind works, you might want to read this. (That is, after all, what they had us read it for). You also might enjoy it if you're a fan of the show <i>Mad Men</i>. I find that I appreciated and enjoyed this book more because of my love of <i>Mad Men</i>, and it helped get me through the more boring parts when I was asking myself, frustrated "Why the fuck am I reading this again???" </span>

<div id="review_508930792" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/508930792" />
    <a title="Chung Chin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7328346-chung-chin"><img alt="Chung Chin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1325724721p2/7328346.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/508930792?book_show_action=true">Jan 17, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chung Chin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chung Chin" href="/user/show/7328346-chung-chin">Chung Chin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7328346-chung-chin?shelf=becoming-smarter">becoming-smarter</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer508930792" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5748809171312310790">I agree with other reviewers who commented that the book is in a need of an update - urgently. <br/><br/>I also didn't particularly enjoy the book because of:<br/>1. The style of writing. <br/>The staccato-styled writing is very distracting for me. Of course, I understand that this is a personal grouch. <br/>2. Repetition on the importance of being first and a very good name. <br/>What happens to those that are not first to market? While there's a section that explains how to position as a follower, I feel that the book is E</span>
  <span id="freeText5748809171312310790" style="display:none">I agree with other reviewers who commented that the book is in a need of an update - urgently. <br /><br />I also didn't particularly enjoy the book because of:<br />1. The style of writing. <br />The staccato-styled writing is very distracting for me. Of course, I understand that this is a personal grouch. <br />2. Repetition on the importance of being first and a very good name. <br />What happens to those that are not first to market? While there's a section that explains how to position as a follower, I feel that the book is EXTREMELY biased such that the author thinks the importance of being first to market cannot be trumped. I agree that being first to market has its advantages, but there's really one slot for number one. So, now what happens to the rest? I don't think the author did a very good job explaining how to position as a follower. It reads more like a "Well, I'm sure someone will ask this question. Let's see if I can get done with it with a few sentences." And then the author harps about the importance of having a good name, but doesn't go into details on how to name a good name. <br /><br />So I won't be highly recommending the book to anyone. If anyone disagree, I'm really open to see what kind of lessons they have learned and successfully implemented for their business. Until then, this remains a big chunk of wasted time for me. </span>
  <a data-text-id="5748809171312310790" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('jj/usCjtIWOOVKSXtukuq3fjYYPLwy5HpWvm6Ym7roR37fVEaDIf1Z1jDdlEaS+sJK47wRrJ+XfPL1QhEbrmHA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

  }

Are you ready for that?&quot; --From the Foreword by Malcolm Gladwell, author of The Tipping Point.Counter to traditional marketing wisdom, which tries to count, measure, and manipulate the spread of information, Seth Godin argues that the information can spread most effectively from customer to customer, rather than from business to customer. Godin calls this powerful customer-to-customer dialogue the ideavirus, and cheerfully eggs marketers on to create an environment where their ideas can replicate and spread. In lively detail, Godin looks at the ways companies such as PayPal, Hotmail, GeoCities, even Volkswagen have successfully launched ideaviruses. He offers a &quot;recipe&quot; for creating your own ideavirus, identifies the key factors in the successful spread of an ideavirus (powerful sneezers, hives, a clear vector, a smooth, friction-free transmission), and shows how any business, large or small, can use ideavirus marketing to succeed in a world that just doesn\'t want to hear it anymore from the traditional marketers.<\/span>\n  <a They have fought for competitive advantage, battled over market share, and struggled for differentiation. Yet, as this influential and immensely popular book shows, these hallmarks of competitive strategy are not the way to create profitable growth in the future.In the international bestseller Blue Ocean Strategy, W. Chan Kim and Renee Mauborgne argue that cutthroat competition results in nothing but a bloody red ocean of rivals fighting over a shrinking profit pool. Based on a study of 150 strategic moves (spanning more than 100 years across 30 industries), the authors argue that lasting success comes not from battling competitors, but from creating &quot;blue oceans&quot;—untapped new market spaces ripe for growth. Such strategic moves, which the authors call “value innovation,” create powerful leaps in value that often render rivals obsolete for more than a decade.Blue Ocean Strategy presents a systematic approach to making the competition irrelevant and outlines principles and tools any company can use to create and capture their own blue oceans. A landmark work that upends traditional thinking about strategy, this bestselling business book charts a bold new path to winning the future.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"10099477864929963337\"           <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/1780.Al_Ries"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1359861563p3/1780.jpg);'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/1780.Al_Ries">Al Ries
</a></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer3565608848310563824">Al Ries is a marketing professional and author. He is also the co-founder and chairman of the Atlanta-based consulting firm Ries & Ries with his partner and daughter, Laura Ries. Along with Jack Trout, Ries coined the term "positioning", as related to the field of marketing, and authored Positioning: The Battle For Your Mind, an industry standard on the subject.<br/>Ries graduated from DePauw Universit</span>
  <span id="freeText3565608848310563824" style="display:none">Al Ries is a marketing professional and author. He is also the co-founder and chairman of the Atlanta-based consulting firm Ries &amp; Ries with his partner and daughter, Laura Ries. Along with Jack Trout, Ries coined the term "positioning", as related to the field of marketing, and authored Positioning: The Battle For Your Mind, an industry standard on the subject.<br />Ries graduated from DePauw University in 1950 with a degree in liberal arts and accepted a position with the advertising department of General Electric before founding his own advertising agency in New York City, Ries Cappiello Colwell, in 1963. The agency later changed to a marketing strategy firm, Trout &amp; Ries.<br />In 1972, again cooperating with Trout, Ries co-authored a three-part series of articles declaring the arrival of the "Positioning Era" in Advertising Age magazine, promoting their concept of positioning.<br />In 1994, Ries founded Ries &amp; Ries with his daughter Laura a recent graduate of Northwestern University and TBWA advertising account executive.<br />Ries was selected as one of the most influential people in the field of public relations in the 20th century by PR Week magazine in 1999. Ries has also written a number of books that have made the BusinessWeek best seller list on a number of occasions.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3565608848310563824" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
